					<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading mw-first-heading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Text messaging</span></h1>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Act of typing and sending a brief, digital message</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Txt msg" redirects here. For the TV series, see <a href="/wiki/Pop-Up_Video" title="Pop-Up Video">Pop-Up Video</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Texter" redirects here. For the surname, see <a href="/wiki/Texter_(surname)" title="Texter (surname)">Texter (surname)</a>.</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For chatting in general, see <a href="/wiki/Online_chat" title="Online chat">online chat</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png/220px-SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="242" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png/330px-SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png/440px-SMS_composition_on_feature_phone.png 2x" data-file-width="583" data-file-height="641" /></a><figcaption>A text message using <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">SMS</a> – the 160 character limit and difficulty of typing on <a href="/wiki/Feature_phone" title="Feature phone">feature phone</a> keypads led to the abbreviations of "<a href="/wiki/SMS_language" title="SMS language">SMS language</a>"</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:LolTextMessage.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/LolTextMessage.jpg/220px-LolTextMessage.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="123" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/LolTextMessage.jpg/330px-LolTextMessage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/LolTextMessage.jpg 2x" data-file-width="344" data-file-height="192" /></a><figcaption>The word "<a href="/wiki/Lol" class="mw-redirect" title="Lol">lol</a>" sent via <a href="/wiki/IMessage" title="IMessage">iMessage</a></figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Text messaging</b>, or <b>texting</b>, is the act of composing and sending electronic messages, typically consisting of alphabetic and numeric characters, between two or more users of <a href="/wiki/Mobile_device" title="Mobile device">mobile devices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desktop_computer" title="Desktop computer">desktops</a>/<a href="/wiki/Laptop" title="Laptop">laptops</a>, or another type of compatible computer. Text messages may be sent over a <a href="/wiki/Cellular_network" title="Cellular network">cellular network</a> or may also be sent via <a href="/wiki/Satellite_phone" title="Satellite phone">satellite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> connection.
</p><p>The term originally referred to messages sent using the <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">Short Message Service</a> (SMS). It has grown beyond alphanumeric text to include multimedia messages using the <a href="/wiki/Multimedia_Messaging_Service" title="Multimedia Messaging Service">Multimedia Messaging Service</a> (MMS), which can contain digital images, videos, and sound content, as well as <a href="/wiki/Ideogram" title="Ideogram">ideograms</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Emoji" title="Emoji">emoji</a> (<a href="/wiki/Smiley" title="Smiley">happy faces</a>, sad faces, and other icons), and <a href="/wiki/Instant_messenger" class="mw-redirect" title="Instant messenger">instant messenger</a> applications (usually the term is used when on mobile devices).
</p><p>Text messages are used for personal, family, business, and social purposes. Governmental and non-governmental organizations use text messaging for communication between colleagues. In the 2010s, the sending of short informal messages became an accepted part of many cultures, as happened earlier with <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">emailing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> This makes texting a quick and easy way to communicate with friends, family, and colleagues, including in contexts where a <a href="/wiki/Telephone_call" title="Telephone call">call</a> would be impolite or inappropriate (e.g., calling very late at night or when one knows the other person is busy with family or work activities). Like e-mail and <a href="/wiki/Voicemail" title="Voicemail">voicemail</a>, and unlike calls (in which the caller hopes to speak directly with the recipient), texting does not require the caller and recipient to both be free at the same moment; this permits communication even between busy individuals. Text messages can also be used to interact with automated systems, for example, to order products or services from <a href="/wiki/E-commerce" title="E-commerce">e-commerce</a> websites or to participate in online contests. Advertisers and service providers use <a href="/wiki/Direct_Text_Marketing" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct Text Marketing">direct text marketing</a> to send messages to mobile users about promotions, payment due dates, and other notifications instead of using <a href="/wiki/Mail" title="Mail">postal mail</a>, <a href="/wiki/Email" title="Email">email</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Voicemail" title="Voicemail">voicemail</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />

href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2023</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The service is referred to by different <a href="/wiki/Colloquialism" title="Colloquialism">colloquialisms</a> depending on the region. It may simply be referred to as a "text" in North America, the United Kingdom, Australia, New Zealand, and the Philippines; an "SMS" in most of mainland Europe; or an "MMS" or "SMS" in the Middle East, Africa, and Asia. The sender of a text message is commonly referred to as a "texter".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Electrical_telegraph" title="Electrical telegraph">electrical telegraph</a> systems, developed in the early 19th century, used electrical signals to send text messages. In the late 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Wireless_telegraphy" title="Wireless telegraphy">wireless telegraphy</a> was developed using <a href="/wiki/Radio_waves" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio waves">radio waves</a>.
</p><p>In 1933, the German <i><a href="/wiki/Reichspost" title="Reichspost">Reichspost</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Reich" title="Reich">Reich</a> postal service) introduced the first "<a href="/wiki/Telex" title="Telex">telex</a>" service.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The University of Hawaii began using radio to send digital information as early as 1971, using <a href="/wiki/ALOHAnet" title="ALOHAnet">ALOHAnet</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedhelm_Hillebrand" title="Friedhelm Hillebrand">Friedhelm Hillebrand</a> conceptualised SMS in 1984 while working for <a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Telekom" title="Deutsche Telekom">Deutsche Telekom</a>. Sitting at a typewriter at home, Hillebrand typed out random sentences and counted every letter, number, punctuation mark, and space. Almost every time, the messages contained fewer than 160 characters, thus giving the basis for the limit one could type via text messaging.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Ghillebaert" title="Bernard Ghillebaert">Bernard Ghillebaert</a> of <a href="/wiki/France_T%C3%A9l%C3%A9com" class="mw-redirect" title="France Télécom">France Télécom</a>, he developed a proposal for the <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a> (Groupe Spécial Mobile) meeting in February 1985 in Oslo.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The first technical solution evolved in a GSM subgroup under the leadership of Finn Trosby. It was further developed under the <a href="/wiki/Leadership" title="Leadership">leadership</a> of Kevin Holley and Ian Harris (see <a href="/wiki/SMS" title="SMS">Short Message Service</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> SMS forms an integral part of <a href="/wiki/Signalling_System_No._7" title="Signalling System No. 7">Signalling System No. 7</a> (SS7).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Under SS7, it is a "state" with 160 characters of data, coded in the ITU-T "T.56" text format, that has a "sequence lead in" to determine different language codes and may have special character codes that permit, for example, sending simple graphs as text. This was part of ISDN (<a href="/wiki/Integrated_Services_Digital_Network" title="Integrated Services Digital Network">Integrated Services Digital Network</a>), and since <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a> is based on this, it made its way to the mobile phone. Messages could be sent and received on ISDN phones, and these can send SMS to any GSM phone. The possibility of doing something is one thing; implementing it is another, but systems existed in 1988 that sent SMS messages to mobile phones<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (compare <a href="/wiki/ND-NOTIS" title="ND-NOTIS">ND-NOTIS</a>).
</p><p>SMS messaging was used for the first time on 3 December 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> at the Three Tuns Public House in Reading, Berkshire,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> when <a href="/wiki/Neil_Papworth" title="Neil Papworth">Neil Papworth</a>, a 22-year-old test engineer for <a href="/wiki/Sema_Group" title="Sema Group">Sema Group</a> in the UK<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> (now <a href="/wiki/Airwide_Solutions" title="Airwide Solutions">Airwide Solutions</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> used a personal computer to send the text message "<a href="/wiki/Merry_Christmas" class="mw-redirect" title="Merry Christmas">Merry Christmas</a>" via the <a href="/wiki/Vodafone" title="Vodafone">Vodafone</a> network to the phone of Richard Jarvis,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-spell_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spell-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> who was at a party in <a href="/wiki/Newbury,_Berkshire" title="Newbury, Berkshire">Newbury, Berkshire</a>, which had been organized to celebrate the event. Modern SMS text messaging is usually sent from one mobile phone to another. Finnish <a href="/wiki/Radiolinja" title="Radiolinja">Radiolinja</a> became the first network to offer a commercial person-to-person SMS text messaging service in 1994. When Radiolinja's domestic competitor, Telecom Finland (now part of <a href="/wiki/TeliaSonera" class="mw-redirect" title="TeliaSonera">TeliaSonera</a>), also launched SMS text messaging in 1995 and the two networks offered cross-network SMS functionality, Finland became the first nation where SMS text messaging was offered on a competitive as well as a commercial basis. GSM was allowed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (February 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in the United States, but the radio frequencies were blocked and awarded to US "Carriers" to use US technology, which limited development of mobile messaging services in the US. The GSM in the US had to use a frequency allocated for <a href="/wiki/Personal_Communications_Service" title="Personal Communications Service">private communication services</a> (PCS) – what the <a href="/wiki/International_Telecommunication_Union" title="International Telecommunication Union">ITU</a> frequency régime had blocked for DECT (<a href="/wiki/Digital_Enhanced_Cordless_Telecommunications" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital Enhanced Cordless Telecommunications">Digital Enhanced Cordless Telecommunications</a>) – a 1000-foot range picocell, but it survived. American Personal Communications (APC), the first GSM carrier in America, provided the first text-messaging service in the United States. Sprint Telecommunications Venture, a partnership of Sprint Corp. and three large cable-TV companies, owned 49 percent of APC. The Sprint venture was the largest single buyer at a government-run spectrum auction that raised $7.7 billion in 2005 for PCS licenses. APC operated under the brand name Sprint Spectrum and launched its service on 15 November 1995, in Washington, D.C., and Baltimore, Maryland. Vice President <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> in Washington, D.C., made the initial phone call to launch the network, calling Mayor <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Schmoke" title="Kurt Schmoke">Kurt Schmoke</a> in Baltimore.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Initial growth of text messaging worldwide was slow, with customers in 1995 sending on average only 0.4 messages per GSM customer per month.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> One factor in the slow take-up of SMS was that operators were slow to set up charging systems, especially for prepaid subscribers, and to eliminate billing fraud, which was possible by changing <a href="/wiki/Short_Message_service_center" title="Short Message service center">SMSC</a> settings on individual handsets to use the SMSCs of other operators.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Over time, this issue was eliminated by switch billing instead of billing at the SMSC and by new features within SMSCs that allowed the blocking of foreign mobile users sending messages through them.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> SMS is available on a wide range of networks, including <a href="/wiki/3G" title="3G">3G</a> networks. However, not all text-messaging systems use SMS; some notable alternate implementations of the concept include <a href="/wiki/SoftBank_Mobile" class="mw-redirect" title="SoftBank Mobile">J-Phone</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=SkyMail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SkyMail (page does not exist)">SkyMail</a> and <a href="/wiki/NTT_Docomo" title="NTT Docomo">NTT Docomo</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=Short_Mail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Short Mail (page does not exist)">Short Mail</a>, both in Japan. E-mail messaging from phones, as popularized by NTT Docomo's <a href="/wiki/I-mode" title="I-mode">i-mode</a> and the RIM <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry" title="BlackBerry">BlackBerry</a>, also typically use standard mail protocols such as <a href="/wiki/Simple_Mail_Transfer_Protocol" title="Simple Mail Transfer Protocol">SMTP</a> over <a href="/wiki/Internet_protocol_suite" title="Internet protocol suite">TCP/IP</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> As of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, text messaging was the most widely used mobile data service, with 74% of all mobile phone users worldwide, or 2.4 billion out of 3.3 billion phone subscribers, being active users of the Short Message Service at the end of 2007. In countries such as Finland, Sweden, and Norway, over 85% of the population used SMS. The European average was about 80%, and North America was rapidly catching up, with over 60% active users of SMS by end of 2008<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The largest average usage of the service by mobile phone subscribers occurs in the Philippines, with an average of 27 texts sent per day per subscriber.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg/220px-IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg/330px-IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg/440px-IPhone_Text_Message_Amber_Alert_1882467856_o.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a><figcaption>A text message on an <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a> announcing an <a href="/wiki/AMBER_Alert" class="mw-redirect" title="AMBER Alert">AMBER Alert</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Text messaging is most often used between private mobile phone users, as a substitute for voice calls in situations where voice communication is impossible or undesirable (e.g., during a school class or a work meeting). Texting is also used to communicate very brief messages, such as informing someone that you will be late or reminding a friend or colleague about a meeting. As with e-mail, informality and brevity have become an accepted part of text messaging. Some text messages such as SMS can also be used for the remote control of home appliances. It is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Domotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Domotics">domotics</a> systems. Some amateurs have also built their own systems to control (some of) their appliances via SMS.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/SMS#Flash_SMS" title="SMS">Flash SMS</a> is a type<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> of text message that appears directly on the main screen without user interaction and is not automatically stored in the inbox. It can be useful in cases such as an emergency (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Fire_alarm_system" title="Fire alarm system">fire alarm</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Confidentiality" title="Confidentiality">confidentiality</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/One-time_password" title="One-time password">one-time password</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>SMS has historically been particularly popular in Europe, Asia (excluding Japan; see below), the United States, Australia, and New Zealand, while also gaining influence in Africa. Popularity has grown to a sufficient extent that the term <i>texting</i> (used as a <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verb</a> meaning the act of mobile phone users sending short messages back and forth) has entered the common lexicon. In 2012, young Asians considered SMS as the most popular mobile phone application.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In the same year, 50 percent of American teens send 50 text messages or more per day, making it their most frequent form of communication.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In 2004 in China, SMS was very popular and brought service providers significant profit (18 billion short messages were sent in 2001).<sup id="cite_ref-2001_message_rate_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2001_message_rate-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been a very influential and powerful tool in the Philippines, where in 2008 the average user sent 10–12 text messages a day. The same year, the Philippines alone sent on average over 1 billion text messages a day,<sup id="cite_ref-Philippine_Daily_Inquirer_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philippine_Daily_Inquirer-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> more than the annual average SMS volume of the countries in Europe, and even China and India. SMS saw hugely popular in India, where youngsters often exchanged many text messages, and companies provide alerts, infotainment, news, cricket scores updates, railway/airline booking, mobile billing, and banking services on SMS.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Similarly, in 2008, text messaging played a primary role in the implication of former Detroit Mayor <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Kilpatrick" title="Kwame Kilpatrick">Kwame Kilpatrick</a> in an SMS sex scandal.<sup id="cite_ref-huliq1_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huliq1-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Short messages are particularly popular among young urbanites.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In many markets, the service is comparatively cheap. For example, in Australia, a message typically costs between <a href="/wiki/Australian_dollar" title="Australian dollar">A$</a>0.20 and $0.25 to send (some prepaid services charge $0.01 between their own phones), compared with a voice call, which costs somewhere between $0.40 and $2.00 per minute (commonly charged in half-minute blocks).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The service is enormously profitable to the service providers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> At a typical length of only 190&#160;bytes (including protocol overhead), more than 350 of these messages per minute can be transmitted at the same data rate as a usual voice call (9&#160;kbit/s). There are also free SMS services available, which are often sponsored, that allow sending<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> and receiving<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> SMS from a <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">PC</a> connected to the Internet. Mobile service providers in New Zealand, such as <a href="/wiki/One_NZ" title="One NZ">One NZ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spark_New_Zealand" title="Spark New Zealand">Spark New Zealand</a>, provided up to 2000 SMS messages for NZ$10 per month. Users on these plans sent on average 1500 SMS messages every month.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Text messaging became so popular that advertising agencies and advertisers jumped into the text messaging business.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Services that provide bulk text message sending are also becoming a popular way for clubs, associations, and advertisers to reach a group of opt-in subscribers quickly.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 2013, research suggested that Internet-based mobile messaging would grow to equal the popularity of SMS by the end of 2013, with nearly 10 trillion messages being sent through each technology.<sup id="cite_ref-Tyntec_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tyntec-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mobilenewscwp_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mobilenewscwp-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Services such as <a href="/wiki/Facebook_Messenger" class="mw-redirect" title="Facebook Messenger">Facebook Messenger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snapchat" title="Snapchat">Snapchat</a>, <a href="/wiki/WhatsApp" title="WhatsApp">WhatsApp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viber" title="Viber">Viber</a> have led to a decline in the use of SMS in parts of the world.
</p><p>Research conducted in 2012 showed that women are more likely than men to use <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">emoticons</a> in text messages.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microblogging">Microblogging</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Microblogging"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Microblogging" title="Microblogging">Microblogging</a></div>
<p>Of many texting trends, a system known as microblogging has surfaced, which consists of a miniaturized blog, inspired mainly by people's tendency to jot down informal thoughts and post them online. They consist of websites like X (formerly <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>) and its Chinese equivalent <a href="/wiki/Microblogging_in_China" title="Microblogging in China">Weibo</a> (微博). As of 2016, 21% of all American adults used Twitter.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> As of 2017, Weibo had 340 million active users.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergency_services">Emergency services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Emergency services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In some countries, text messages can be used to contact emergency services. In the UK, text messages can be used to call emergency services only after registering with the emergency SMS service. This service is primarily aimed at people who, because of disability, are unable to make a voice call. It has recently been promoted as a means for walkers and climbers to call<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> emergency services from areas where a voice call is not possible due to low signal strength.
</p><p>In the US, there is a move to require both traditional operators and <a href="/wiki/Over-the-top_content" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-the-top content">Over-the-top</a> messaging providers to support texting to 911.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> In Asia, SMS is used for tsunami warnings and in Europe, SMS is used to inform individuals of imminent disasters. Since the location of a handset is known, systems can alert everyone in an area that the events have made impossible to pass through e.g. an avalanche. A similar system, known as <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Alert_Australia" title="Emergency Alert Australia">Emergency Alert</a>, is used in Australia to notify the public of impending disasters through both SMS and landline phone calls. These messages can be sent based on either the location of the phone or the address to which the handset is registered.
</p><p>In the early 2020s, device manufacturers have begun to integrate <a href="/wiki/Satellite_phone#Integration_into_conventional_mobile_phones" title="Satellite phone">satellite messaging</a> connectivity and satellite emergency services into conventional mobile phones for use in remote regions, where there is no reliable terrestrial cellular network.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reminders_of_medical_appointments">Reminders of medical appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reminders of medical appointments"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>SMS messages are used in some countries as reminders of medical appointments. Missed outpatient clinic appointments cost the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service_(England)" title="National Health Service (England)">National Health Service (England)</a> more than £600 million ($980 million) a year.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> SMS messages are thought to be more cost-effective, swifter to deliver, and more likely to receive a faster response than letters. A 2012 study by Sims and colleagues examined the outcomes of 24,709 outpatient appointments scheduled in mental health services in South-East London. The study found that SMS message reminders could reduce the number of missed psychiatric appointments by 25–28%, representing a potential national yearly saving of over £150 million.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the COVID-19 pandemic, medical facilities in the United States are using text messaging to coordinate the appointment process, including reminders, cancellations, and safe check-in. US-based cloud <a href="/wiki/Radiological_information_system" title="Radiological information system">radiology information system</a> vendor AbbaDox includes this in their patient engagement services.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_uses">Commercial uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Commercial uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg/220px-Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="228" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg/330px-Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg/440px-Ejemplo_de_Mensaje_Multimedia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1708" data-file-height="1768" /></a><figcaption>A multimedia message displayed on a mobile phone</figcaption></figure>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Short_codes">Short codes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Short codes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Short_codes" class="mw-redirect" title="Short codes">Short codes</a> are special telephone numbers, shorter than full telephone numbers, that can be used to address SMS and MMS messages from mobile phones or fixed phones. There are two types of short codes: dialling and messaging.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Text_messaging_gateway_providers">Text messaging gateway providers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Text messaging gateway providers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/SMS_gateway" title="SMS gateway">SMS gateway</a> providers facilitate the SMS traffic between businesses and mobile subscribers, being mainly responsible for carrying mission-critical messages, SMS for enterprises, content delivery and entertainment services involving SMS, e.g., TV voting. Considering SMS messaging performance and cost, as well as the level of text messaging services, SMS gateway providers can be classified as resellers of the text messaging capability of another provider's <a href="/wiki/Short_Message_Service_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Short Message Service Center">SMSC</a> or offering the text messaging capability as an operator of their own SMSC with <a href="/wiki/Signalling_System_No._7" title="Signalling System No. 7">SS7</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> SMS messaging gateway providers can provide gateway-to-mobile (Mobile Terminated–MT) services. Some suppliers can also supply mobile-to-gateway (text-in or Mobile Originated/MO services). Many operate text-in services on short codes or mobile number ranges, whereas others use lower-cost geographic text-in numbers.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Premium_content">Premium content</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Premium content"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>SMS has been widely used for delivering digital content, such as news alerts, financial information, pictures, GIFs, logos and ringtones.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Such messages are also known as premium-rated short messages (PSMS).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> The subscribers are charged extra for receiving this premium content, and the amount is typically divided between the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_network_operator" title="Mobile network operator">mobile network operator</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Value-added_service" title="Value-added service">value added service provider</a> (VASP), either through revenue share or a fixed transport fee. Services like <a href="/wiki/Texperts" title="Texperts">82ASK</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=63336&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="63336 (page does not exist)">Any Question Answered</a> have used the PSMS model to enable rapid response to mobile consumers' questions, using on-call teams of experts and researchers. In November 2013, amidst complaints about unsolicited charges on bills, major mobile carriers in the US agreed to stop billing for PSMS in 45 states, effectively ending its use in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside the United States, premium short messages have been used for "real-world" services. For example, some vending machines now allow payment by sending a premium-rated short message, so that the cost of the item bought is added to the user's phone bill or subtracted from the user's prepaid credits. Recently,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (November 2023)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> premium messaging companies have come under fire from consumer groups due to a large number of consumers racking up huge phone bills. A new type of free-premium or hybrid-premium content has emerged with the launch of text-service websites. These sites allow registered users to receive free text messages when items they are interested in go on sale, or when new items are introduced. An alternative to inbound SMS is based on <a href="/wiki/Long_number" title="Long number">long numbers</a> (international mobile number format, e.g., +44 7624 805000, or geographic numbers that can handle voice and SMS, e.g., 01133203040<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>), which can be used in place of short codes or premium-rated short messages for SMS reception in several applications, such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=TV_voting&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="TV voting (page does not exist)">TV voting</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> product promotions and campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Long numbers are internationally available, as well as enabling businesses to have their own number, rather than short codes, which are usually shared across a lot of brands. Additionally, long numbers are non-premium inbound numbers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_workplaces">In workplaces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: In workplaces"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The use of text messaging for workplace purposes grew significantly during the mid-2000s. As companies seek competitive advantages, many employees used new technology, collaborative applications, and real-time messaging such as SMS, <a href="/wiki/Instant_messaging" title="Instant messaging">instant messaging</a>, and mobile communications to connect with teammates and customers. Some practical uses of text messaging include the use of SMS for confirming delivery or other tasks, for instant communication between a service provider and a client (e.g., a <a href="/wiki/Payment_card_industry" title="Payment card industry">payment card company</a> and a consumer), and for sending alerts. Several universities have implemented a system of texting students and faculties campus alerts. One such example is <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Penn State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As text messaging has proliferated in business, so too have regulations governing its use. One regulation specifically governing the use of text messaging in financial-services firms engaged in stocks, equities, and securities trading is <i>Regulatory Notice 07-59, Supervision of Electronic Communications, December 2007</i>, issued to member firms by the <a href="/wiki/Financial_Industry_Regulatory_Authority" title="Financial Industry Regulatory Authority">Financial Industry Regulatory Authority</a> (FINRA). In Regulatory Notice 07-59, FINRA noted that "electronic communications", "e-mail", and "electronic correspondence" may be used interchangeably and can include such forms of electronic messaging as instant messaging and text messaging.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Industry has had to develop new technology to allow companies to archive their employees' text messages.
</p><p>Security, confidentiality, reliability, and speed of SMS are among the most important guarantees industries such as financial services, energy and commodities trading, health care and enterprises demand in their mission-critical procedures. One way to guarantee such a quality of text messaging lies in introducing SLAs (<a href="/wiki/Service_Level_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="Service Level Agreement">Service Level Agreement</a>), which are common in IT contracts. By providing measurable SLAs, corporations can define reliability parameters and set up a high quality of their services.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Just one of many SMS applications that have proven highly popular and successful in the financial services industry is mobile receipts. In January 2009, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mobile_Marketing_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mobile Marketing Association (page does not exist)">Mobile Marketing Association</a> (MMA) published the <i>Mobile Banking Overview</i> for financial institutions in which it discussed the advantages and disadvantages of mobile channel platforms such as Short Message Services (SMS), Mobile Web, Mobile Client Applications, SMS with Mobile Web and Secure SMS.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mobile interaction services are an alternative way of using SMS in business communications with greater certainty. Typical business-to-business applications are <a href="/wiki/Telematics" title="Telematics">telematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Machine-to-Machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Machine-to-Machine">Machine-to-Machine</a>, in which two applications automatically communicate with each other. Incident alerts are also common, and staff communications are also another use for B2B scenarios. Businesses can use SMS for time-critical alerts, updates, and reminders, mobile campaigns, content and entertainment applications. Mobile interaction can also be used for consumer-to-business interactions, such as media voting and competitions, and consumer-to-consumer interaction, for example, with mobile social networking, chatting and dating.
</p><p>Text messaging is widely used in business settings; as well, it is used in many <a href="/wiki/Civil_service" title="Civil service">civil service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organization</a> workplaces. The U.S. And Canadian civil service both adopted <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry" title="BlackBerry">BlackBerry</a> smartphones in the 2000s.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Group_texts">Group texts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Group texts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Group texts involve more than two users. They are often used when it is helpful to message many people at once, such as inviting multiple people to an event or arranging groups.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> They are also used in business for marketing and other customer notifications as well as intracompany communication.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Group texts are often sent as MMS messages and therefore require an internet connection to send instead of using the sender's text messaging plan.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_SMS_services">Online SMS services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Online SMS services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are a growing number of websites that allow users to send free SMS messages online. Some websites provide free SMS for promoting premium business packages.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide_use">Worldwide use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Worldwide use"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Europe"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg/220px-SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="105" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg/330px-SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg/440px-SMS_roaming_welcome_messages_-_Redvers.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1564" data-file-height="744" /></a><figcaption>SMS is used to send "welcome" messages to mobile phones <a href="/wiki/Roaming" title="Roaming">roaming</a> between countries. Here, <a href="/wiki/T-Mobile_International_AG" title="T-Mobile International AG">T-Mobile</a> welcomes a <a href="/wiki/Proximus" title="Proximus">Proximus</a> subscriber to the UK, and <a href="/wiki/Base_(mobile_telephony_provider)" title="Base (mobile telephony provider)">Base</a> welcomes an <a href="/wiki/Orange_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange United Kingdom">Orange</a> UK customer to Belgium.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In 2003, Europe followed next behind Asia in terms of the popularity of the use of SMS. That year, an average of 16 billion messages were sent each month. Users in Spain sent a little more than fifty messages per month on average in 2003. In Italy, Germany and the United Kingdom, the figure was around 35–40 SMS messages per month. In each of these countries, the cost of sending an SMS message varied from €0.04–0.23, depending on the payment plan (with many contractual plans including all or several texts for free). In the United Kingdom, text messages are charged between £0.05–0.12. Curiously, France did not take to SMS in the same way, sending just under 20 messages on average per user per month. France has the same <a href="/wiki/GSM" title="GSM">GSM</a> technology as other European countries, so the uptake is not hampered by technical restrictions.
</p><p>In the Republic of Ireland, in 2012, 1.5 billion messages were sent every quarter, on average 114 messages per person per month.<sup id="cite_ref-RTE_report_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RTE_report-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the United Kingdom, as of March&#160;2012,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> over 1 billion text messages were sent every week.<sup id="cite_ref-Mobile_Data_Association_UK_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mobile_Data_Association_UK-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest" title="Eurovision Song Contest">Eurovision Song Contest</a> organized the first pan-European SMS voting in 2002, as a part of the voting system (there was also a voting over traditional landline phone lines). In 2005, the Eurovision Song Contest organized the biggest televoting ever (with SMS and phone voting). During <a href="/wiki/Roaming" title="Roaming">roaming</a>, (that is, when a user connects to another network in different country from their own) the prices may be higher, but in July 2009, EU legislation went into effect limiting this price to €0.11.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mobile service providers in Finland offered contracts in which users can send 1000 text messages a month for €10. In Finland, which has very high mobile phone ownership rates,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> some TV channels began "SMS chat", which involved sending short messages to a phone number, and the messages would be shown on TV. Chats are always moderated, which prevents users from sending offensive material to the channel. The craze evolved into quizzes and strategy games and then faster-paced games designed for television and SMS control. Games require users to register their nicknames and send short messages to control a character onscreen. Messages usually cost 0.05 to 0.86 Euro apiece, and games can require the player to send dozens of messages. In December 2003, a Finnish TV channel, <a href="/wiki/MTV3" title="MTV3">MTV3</a>, put a <a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus" title="Santa Claus">Santa Claus</a> character on-air reading aloud text messages sent in by viewers. On 12 March 2004, the first entirely "interactive" TV channel, VIISI, began operation in Finland. However, SBS Finland Oy took over the channel and turned it into a music channel named <i>The Voice</i> in November 2004. In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Finland" title="Prime Minister of Finland">Prime Minister of Finland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matti_Vanhanen" title="Matti Vanhanen">Matti Vanhanen</a>, made the news when he allegedly broke up with his girlfriend with a text message.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 2007, the first book written solely in text messages, <i>Viimeiset viestit</i> (<i>Last Messages</i>), was released by Finnish author <a href="/wiki/Hannu_Luntiala" title="Hannu Luntiala">Hannu Luntiala</a>. It is about an executive who travels through Europe and India.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: United States"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the United States, text messaging is very popular; as reported by <a href="/wiki/CTIA_(organization)" title="CTIA (organization)">CTIA</a> in December 2009, the 286 million US subscribers sent 152.7 billion text messages per month, for an average of 534 messages per subscriber per month.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> found in May 2010 that 72% of U.S. adult cellphone users send and receive text messages.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> CTIA reported in 2022 that 2 trillion SMS and MMS were sent in the United States in 2021, showing continued popularity of the technology.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the U.S., SMS is often charged both at the sender and at the destination, but, unlike phone calls, it cannot be rejected or dismissed. The reasons for lower uptake than other countries are varied. Many users have unlimited "mobile-to-mobile" minutes, high monthly minute allotments, or unlimited service. Moreover, "<a href="/wiki/Push_to_talk" class="mw-redirect" title="Push to talk">push to talk</a>" services offer the instant connectivity of SMS and are typically unlimited. The integration between competing providers and technologies necessary for cross-network text messaging was not initially available. Some providers originally charged extra for texting, reducing its appeal. In the third quarter of 2006, at least 12 billion text messages were sent on AT&amp;T's network, up almost 15% from the preceding quarter.
</p><p>While texting is mainly popular among people from 13–22 years old, it is also increasing among adults and business users.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The age that a child receives his/her first cell phone has also decreased<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, making text messaging a popular way of communicating. The number of texts sent in the US has gone up over the years as the price has gone down to an average of $0.10 per text sent and received.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> To convince more customers to buy unlimited text messaging plans, some major cellphone providers have increased the price to send and receive text messages from $.15 to $.20 per message.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> This is over $1,300 per <a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">megabyte</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Many providers offer unlimited plans, which can result in a lower rate per text, given sufficient volume.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Japan"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> was among the first countries to adopt short messages widely, with pioneering non-GSM services including <a href="/wiki/SoftBank_Mobile" class="mw-redirect" title="SoftBank Mobile">J-Phone</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=SkyMail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="SkyMail (page does not exist)">SkyMail</a> and <a href="/wiki/NTT_Docomo" title="NTT Docomo">NTT Docomo</a>'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=Short_Mail&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Short Mail (page does not exist)">Short Mail</a>. Japanese adolescents first began text messaging, because it was a cheaper form of communication than the other available forms. Thus, Japanese theorists created the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Selective_interpersonal_relationship_theory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Selective interpersonal relationship theory (page does not exist)">selective interpersonal relationship theory</a>, claiming that mobile phones can change <a href="/wiki/Social_networks" class="mw-redirect" title="Social networks">social networks</a> among young people (classified as 13- to 30-year-olds). They theorized this age group had extensive but low-quality relationships with friends, and mobile phone usage may facilitate improvement in the quality of their relationships. They concluded this age group prefers "selective interpersonal relationships in which they maintain particular, partial, but rich relations, depending on the situation."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The same studies showed participants rated friendships in which they communicated face-to-face and through text messaging as being more intimate than those in which they communicated solely face-to-face. This indicates participants make new relationships with face-to-face communication at an early stage, but use text messaging to increase their contact later on. As the relationships between participants grew more intimate, the frequency of text messaging also increased. However, short messaging has been largely rendered obsolete by the prevalence of mobile Internet e-mail,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> which can be sent to and received from any e-mail address, mobile or otherwise. That said, while usually presented to the user simply as a uniform "mail" service (and most users are unaware of the distinction), the operators may still internally transmit the content as short messages, especially if the destination is on the same network.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: China"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Text messaging has historically been popular and cheap in China. About 700 billion messages were sent in 2007. Text message spam has also been a problem in China. In 2007, 353.8 billion spam messages were sent, up 93% from the previous year. It is about 12.44 messages per week per person. In 2010, it was routine that the People's Republic of China government monitored text messages across the country for illegal content.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
Among Chinese migrant workers with little formal education, it is common to refer to SMS manuals when text messaging. These manuals are published as cheap, smaller-than-pocket-size booklets that offer diverse linguistic phrases to utilize as messages.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philippines">Philippines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Philippines"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>SMS was introduced to selected markets in the Philippines in 1995.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 1998, Philippine mobile service providers launched SMS more widely across the country, with initial television marketing campaigns targeting hearing-impaired users. The service was initially free with subscriptions, but Filipinos quickly exploited the feature to communicate for free instead of using voice calls, which they would be charged for.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> After telephone companies realized this trend, they began charging for SMS. The rate across networks is 1 peso per SMS (about US$0.023). Even after users were charged for SMS, it remained cheap, about one-tenth of the price of a voice call. This low price led to about five million Filipinos owning a cell phone by 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-Rheingold,_Howard_2002_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rheingold,_Howard_2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Because of the highly social nature of Philippine culture and the affordability of SMS compared to voice calls, SMS usage shot up. Filipinos used texting not only for social messages but also for political purposes, as it allowed the Filipinos to express their opinions on current events and political issues.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> It became a powerful tool for Filipinos in promoting or denouncing issues and was a key factor during the 2001 <a href="/wiki/EDSA_II" class="mw-redirect" title="EDSA II">EDSA II</a> revolution, which overthrew then-President <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Estrada" title="Joseph Estrada">Joseph Estrada</a>, who was eventually found guilty of corruption. According to 2009 statistics, there were about 72 million mobile service subscriptions (roughly 80% of the Filipino population), with around 1.39 billion SMS messages being sent daily.<sup id="cite_ref-businesswire.com_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesswire.com-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wayodd.com_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wayodd.com-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Because of the large number of text messages being sent, the Philippines became known as the "text capital of the world" during the late 1990s until the early 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-businesswire.com_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-businesswire.com-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wayodd.com_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wayodd.com-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: New Zealand"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are three mobile network companies operating in New Zealand, with some sub-brands and MVNOs.
<a href="/wiki/Spark_New_Zealand" title="Spark New Zealand">Spark NZ</a> (formerly Telecom NZ), was the first telecommunication company in New Zealand. In 2011, Spark was broken into two companies by regulation, with Chorus Ltd taking the landline infrastructure and Spark NZ providing services including over their mobile network. Vodafone NZ (now <a href="/wiki/One_NZ" title="One NZ">One NZ</a>) acquired mobile network provider Bellsouth New Zealand in 1998 and had 2.32 million customers as of July 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Vodafone launched the first Text messaging service in 1999<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and has introduced innovative TXT services like SafeTXT and CallMe<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2degrees" title="2degrees">2degrees</a> Mobile Ltd launched in August 2009.
In 2005, around 85% of the adult population had a mobile phone.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In general, texting is more popular than making phone calls, as it is viewed as less intrusive and therefore more polite.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sub-Saharan_Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Sub-Saharan Africa"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2009, it was predicted that text messaging would become a key revenue driver for mobile network operators in Africa over the following couple of years.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Today, text messaging is already slowly gaining influence in the African market.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> One such person used text messaging to spread the word about HIV and AIDS.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="The current source is insufficiently reliable (WP:NOTRS). (November 2023)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In September 2009, a multi-country campaign in Africa used text messaging to expose stock-outs of essential medicines at public health facilities and put pressure on governments to address the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_effects">Social effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Social effects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The advent of text messaging made possible new forms of interaction that were not possible before. A person could carry out a conversation with another user without the constraint of being expected to reply within a short amount of time and without needing to set time aside to engage in conversation. With voice calling, both participants need to be free at the same time. Mobile phone users can maintain communication during situations in which a voice call is impractical, impossible, or unacceptable, such as during a school class or work meeting. Texting has provided a venue for <a href="/wiki/Participatory_culture" title="Participatory culture">participatory culture</a>, allowing viewers to vote in online and TV polls, as well as receive information while they are on the move. Texting can also bring people together and create a sense of community through "<a href="/wiki/Smart_mob" title="Smart mob">Smart Mobs</a>" or "Net War", which create "people power".<sup id="cite_ref-Rheingold,_Howard_2002_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rheingold,_Howard_2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> 
Research in 2015 has also proven that text messaging is somehow making the social distances larger and could be ruining verbal communication skills for many people.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_language">Effect on language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Effect on language"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/SMS_language" title="SMS language">SMS language</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SillyModernSociety.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/SillyModernSociety.jpg/220px-SillyModernSociety.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/SillyModernSociety.jpg/330px-SillyModernSociety.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/SillyModernSociety.jpg/440px-SillyModernSociety.jpg 2x" data-file-width="834" data-file-height="693" /></a><figcaption>This sticker seen in Paris satirizes the popularity of communication in SMS shorthand. In French: "Is that you? / It's me! / Do you love me? / Shut up!"</figcaption></figure>
<p>The small <a href="/wiki/Telephone_keypad" title="Telephone keypad">phone keypad</a> and the rapidity of typical text message exchanges have caused a number of spelling abbreviations: as in the phrase "txt msg", "u" (an abbreviation for "you"), "HMU"("hit me up"; i.e., call me), or use of <a href="/wiki/Camel_case" title="Camel case">camel case</a>, such as in "ThisIsVeryLame". To avoid the even more limited message lengths allowed when using <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek</a> letters, speakers of languages written in those alphabets often use the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> for their own <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>. In certain languages utilizing <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritic</a> marks, such as Polish, SMS technology created an entire new variant of written language: characters normally written with diacritic marks (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%84" title="Ą">ą</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/%C4%98" title="Ę">ę</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%9A" title="Ś">ś</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/%C5%BB" title="Ż">ż</a></i> in Polish) are now being written without them (as <i>a</i>, <i>e</i>, <i>s</i>, <i>z</i>) to enable using cell phones without Polish <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">script</a> or to save space in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> messages. Historically, this language developed out of shorthand used in <a href="/wiki/Bulletin_board_system" title="Bulletin board system">bulletin board systems</a> and later in Internet <a href="/wiki/Chat_room" title="Chat room">chat rooms</a>, where users would abbreviate some words to allow a response to be typed more quickly, though the amount of time saved was often inconsequential. However, this became much more pronounced in SMS, where mobile phone users either have a numeric keyboard (with older cellphones) or a small <a href="/wiki/QWERTY" title="QWERTY">QWERTY</a> keyboard (for 2010s-era smartphones), so more effort is required to type each character, and there is sometimes a limit on the number of characters that may be sent. In <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a>, numbers that sound similar to words are used in place of those words. For example, the numbers 520 in Chinese (<i>wǔ èr líng</i>) sound like the words for "I love you" (<i>wǒ ài nǐ</i>). The sequence 748 (<i>qī sì bā</i>) sounds like the curse "go to hell" (<i>qù sǐ ba</i>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Predictive_text" title="Predictive text">Predictive text</a> software, which attempts to guess words (<a href="/wiki/Tegic" title="Tegic">Tegic</a>'s <a href="/wiki/T9_(predictive_text)" title="T9 (predictive text)">T9</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/ITap" title="ITap">iTap</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Letter_(alphabet)" title="Letter (alphabet)">letters</a> (Eatoni's <a href="/wiki/LetterWise" title="LetterWise">LetterWise</a>) reduces the labour of time-consuming input. This makes abbreviations not only less necessary but slower to type than regular words that are in the software's <a href="/wiki/Dictionary" title="Dictionary">dictionary</a>. However, it makes the messages longer, often requiring the text message to be sent in multiple parts and, therefore, costing more to send. The use of text messaging has changed the way that people talk and write essays, some<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> believing it to be harmful. Children today are receiving cell phones at an age as young as eight years old; more than 35 per cent of children in second and third grade have their own mobile phones.
