Because of this, the texting language is integrated into the way that students think from an earlier age than ever before.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In November 2006, New Zealand Qualifications Authority approved the move that allowed students of secondary schools to use mobile phone text language in the end-of-the-year-exam papers.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Highly publicized reports, beginning in 2002, of the use of text language in school assignments, caused some to become concerned that the quality of written communication is on the decline,<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and other reports claim that teachers and professors are beginning to have a hard time controlling the problem.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> However, the notion that text language is widespread or harmful is refuted by research from linguistic experts.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> explores how text messaging has <a href="/wiki/Anglicisation" title="Anglicisation">anglicized</a> some of the world's languages. The use of <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritic</a> marks is dropped in languages such as French, as well as symbols in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Semitic_languages" title="Ethiopian Semitic languages">Ethiopian languages</a>. In his book, <i><a href="/wiki/Txtng:_the_Gr8_Db8" title="Txtng: the Gr8 Db8">Txtng: the Gr8 Db8</a></i> (which translates as "Texting: the Great Debate"), <a href="/wiki/David_Crystal" title="David Crystal">David Crystal</a> states that texters in all eleven languages use "lol" ("laughing out loud"), "u", "brb" ("be right back"), and "gr8" ("great"), all English-based shorthands. The use of pictograms and logograms in texts are present in every language. They shorten words by using symbols to represent the word or symbols whose name sounds like a syllable of the word such as in 2day or b4. This is commonly used in other languages as well. Crystal gives some examples in several languages such as Italian <i>sei</i>, "six", is used for <i>sei</i>, "you are". Example: dv6 = <i>dove sei</i> ("where are you") and French k7 = <i>cassette</i> ("cassette tape"). There is also the use of numeral sequences, substituting for several syllables of a word and creating whole phrases using numerals. For example, in French, a12c4 can be said as <i>à un de ces quatres</i>, "see you around" (literally: "to one of these four <i>[days]</i>"). An example of using symbols in texting and borrowing from English is the use of <i>@</i>. Whenever it is used in texting, its intended use is with the English pronunciation. Crystal gives the example of the Welsh use of <i>@</i> in <i>@F</i>, pronounced ataf, meaning "to me". In character-based languages such as Chinese and Japanese, numbers are assigned syllables based on the shortened form of the pronunciation of the number, sometimes the English pronunciation of the number. In this way, numbers alone can be used to communicate whole passages, such as in Chinese, "8807701314520" (bào bao nǐ qīng qing nǐ yīshēng yìshì wǒ ài nǐ) can be literally translated as "Hug hug you, kiss you, whole life, whole life I love you." English influences worldwide texting in variation, but still in combination with the individual properties of languages.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>American <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a> is also recognized in shorthand. For example, <a href="/wiki/Homer_Simpson" title="Homer Simpson">Homer Simpson</a> translates into: ~(_8^(|).<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Crystal also suggests that texting has led to more creativity in the English language, giving people opportunities to create their own slang, <a href="/wiki/Emoticon" title="Emoticon">emoticons</a>, abbreviations, acronyms, etc. The feeling of individualism and freedom makes texting more popular and a more efficient way to communicate.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Crystal has also been quoted in saying that "In a logical world, text messaging should not have survived." But text messaging didn't just come out of nowhere. It originally began as a messaging system that would send out emergency information. But it gained immediate popularity with the public. What followed is the SMS we see today, which is a very quick and efficient way of sharing information from person to person. Work by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ling" title="Richard Ling">Richard Ling</a> has shown that texting has a gendered dimension and it plays into the development of teen identity.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In addition we text to a very small number of other persons. For most people, half of their texts go to 3 – 5 other people.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research by Rosen et al. (2009)<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> found that those young adults who used more language-based textisms (shortcuts such as LOL, 2nite, etc.) in daily writing produced worse formal writing than those young adults who used fewer linguistic textisms in daily writing. However, the exact opposite was true for informal writing. This suggests that perhaps the act of using textisms to shorten communication words leads young adults to produce more informal writing, which may then help them to be better "informal" writers. Due to text messaging, teens are writing more, and some teachers see that this comfort with language can be harnessed to make better writers. This new form of communication may be encouraging students to put their thoughts and feelings into words and this may be able to be used as a bridge, to get them more interested in formal writing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (November 2015)">according to whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Joan_H._Lee" class="mw-redirect" title="Joan H. Lee">Joan H. Lee</a> in her thesis, <i>What does txting do 2 language: The influences of exposure to messaging and print media on acceptability constraints</i> (2011),<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> she associates exposure to text messaging with more rigid acceptability constraints. The thesis suggests that more exposure to the colloquial, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Generation_text&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Generation text (page does not exist)">Generation Text</a> language of text messaging contributes to being less accepting of words. In contrast, Lee found that students with more exposure to traditional print media (such as books and magazines) were more accepting of both real and fictitious words. The thesis, which garnered international media attention, also presents a <a href="/wiki/Literature_review" title="Literature review">literature review</a> of <a href="/wiki/Academic_publishing" title="Academic publishing">academic literature</a> on the effects of text messaging on language. Texting has also been shown to have had no effect or some positive effects on <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>. According to Plester, Wood and Joshi and their research done on the study of 88 British 10–12-year-old children and their knowledge of text messages, "textisms are essentially forms of phonetic abbreviation" that show that "to produce and read such abbreviations arguably requires a level of phonological awareness (and orthographic awareness) in the child concerned."<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Texting_while_driving">Texting while driving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Texting while driving"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg/220px-Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg/330px-Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg/440px-Cell_phone_use_while_driving.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2032" data-file-height="1524" /></a><figcaption>A driver with attention divided between a mobile phone and the road ahead</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Texting_while_driving" title="Texting while driving">Texting while driving</a></div>
<p>Texting while driving leads to increased distraction behind the wheel and can lead to an increased risk of an accident. In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Mutual_Insurance_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberty Mutual Insurance Group">Liberty Mutual Insurance Group</a> conducted a survey with more than 900 teens from over 26 high schools nationwide. The results showed that 87% of students found texting to be "very" or "extremely" distracting.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> A study by <a href="/wiki/American_Automobile_Association" title="American Automobile Association">AAA</a> found that 46% of teens admitted to being distracted behind the wheel due to texting. One example of distraction behind the wheel is the <a href="/wiki/2008_Chatsworth_train_collision" title="2008 Chatsworth train collision">2008 Chatsworth train collision</a>, which killed 25 passengers. The engineer had sent 45 text messages while operating the train.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A 2009 experiment with <i><a href="/wiki/Car_and_Driver" title="Car and Driver">Car and Driver</a></i> editor Eddie Alterman (that took place at a deserted airfield, for safety reasons) compared texting with <a href="/wiki/Drunk_driving" title="Drunk driving">drunk driving</a>. The experiment found that texting while driving was more dangerous than being drunk. While being legally drunk added 4 feet to Alterman's stopping distance while going 70&#160;mph (110&#160;km/h), reading an e-mail on a phone added 36 feet (11&#160;m), and sending a text message added 70 feet (21&#160;m).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Polytechnic_Institute_and_State_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia Polytechnic Institute and State University">Virginia Tech</a> Transportation Institute released the results of an 18-month study that involved placing cameras inside the cabs of more than 100 long-haul trucks, which recorded the drivers over a combined driving distance of three million miles. The study concluded that when the drivers were texting, their risk of crashing was 23 times greater than when not texting.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Texting_while_walking">Texting while walking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Texting while walking"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to the proliferation of smart phone applications performed while walking, "texting while walking" or "wexting" is the increasing practice of people being transfixed to their mobile device without looking in any direction but their personal screen while walking. First coined reference in 2015 in New York from Rentrak's chief client officer<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> when discussing time spent with media and various media usage metrics. Text messaging among pedestrians leads to increased cognitive distraction and reduced situation awareness, and may lead to increases in unsafe behaviour leading to injury and death.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamberg_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamberg-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Licence2015_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Licence2015-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Recent studies conducted on cell phone use while walking showed that cell phone users recall fewer objects when conversing,<sup id="cite_ref-Nasar_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasar-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> walk slower,<sup id="cite_ref-Lamberg_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamberg-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Lopresti_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopresti-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> have altered gait<sup id="cite_ref-Licence2015_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Licence2015-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uchiyama_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uchiyama-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and are more unsafe when crossing a street.<sup id="cite_ref-Nasar_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nasar-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, some gait analyses showed that stance phase during overstepping motion, longitudinal and lateral deviation increased during cell phone operation, but step length and clearance did not;<sup id="cite_ref-Lamberg_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamberg-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Uchiyama_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uchiyama-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> a different analysis did find increased step clearance and reduced step length.<sup id="cite_ref-Licence2015_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Licence2015-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is unclear which processes may be affected by distraction, which types of distraction may affect which cognitive processes, and how individual differences may affect the influence of distraction.<sup id="cite_ref-Schwebel_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schwebel-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Lamberg and Muratori believe that engaging in a dual-task, such as texting while walking, may interfere with working memory and result in walking errors.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamberg_96-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamberg-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Their study demonstrated that participants engaged in text messaging were unable to maintain walking speed or retain accurate spatial information, suggesting an inability to adequately divide their attention between two tasks. According to them, the addition of texting while walking with vision occluded increases the demands placed on the working memory system resulting in gait disruptions.<sup id="cite_ref-Lamberg_96-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lamberg-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Texting on a phone distracts participants, even when the texting task used is a relatively simple one.<sup id="cite_ref-Lopresti_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopresti-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Stavrinos et al. investigated the effect of other cognitive tasks, such as engaging in conversations or cognitive tasks on a phone, and found that participants actually have reduced visual awareness.<sup id="cite_ref-Stavrinos_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stavrinos-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> This finding was supported by Licence et al., who conducted a similar study.<sup id="cite_ref-Licence2015_97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Licence2015-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> For example, texting pedestrians may fail to notice unusual events in their environment, such as a unicycling clown.<sup id="cite_ref-Hyman_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyman-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> These findings suggest that tasks that require the allocation of cognitive resources can affect visual attention even when the task itself does not require the participants to avert their eyes from their environment. The act of texting itself seems to impair pedestrians' visual awareness. It appears that the distraction produced by texting is a combination of both a cognitive and visual perceptual distraction.<sup id="cite_ref-Lopresti_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lopresti-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> A study conducted by Licence et al. supported some of these findings, particularly that those who text while walking significantly alter their gait. However, they also found that the gait pattern texters adopted was slower and more "protective", and consequently did not increase obstacle contact or tripping in a typical pedestrian context.<sup id="cite_ref-Licence2015_97-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Licence2015-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There have also been technological approaches to increase the safety/awareness of pedestrians that are (unintentionally) blind while using a smartphone, e.g., using a <a href="/wiki/Kinect" title="Kinect">Kinect</a><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> or an ultrasound phone cover<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> as a virtual white cane, or using the built-in camera to algorithmically analyze single,<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> respectively a stream of pictures<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> for obstacles, with Wang et al. proposing to use machine learning to specifically detect incoming vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexting">Sexting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sexting"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexting" title="Sexting">Sexting</a></div>
<p>Sexting is slang for the act of sending sexually explicit or suggestive content between mobile devices using SMS.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Risks_and_Threats_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Risks_and_Threats-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> It contains either text, images, or video that is intended to be sexually arousing. Sexting was reported as early as 2005 in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Telegraph" title="The Sunday Telegraph">The Sunday Telegraph</a> Magazine</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-SunTele_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SunTele-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> constituting a trend in the creative use of SMS to excite another with alluring messages throughout the day.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although sexting often takes place consensually between two people, it can also occur against the wishes of a person who is the subject of the content.<sup id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_of_Risks_and_Threats_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_of_Risks_and_Threats-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> A number of instances have been reported in which the recipients of sexting have shared the content of the messages with others, with less intimate intentions, such as to impress their friends or embarrass their sender. Celebrities such as <a href="/wiki/Miley_Cyrus" title="Miley Cyrus">Miley Cyrus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vanessa_Hudgens" title="Vanessa Hudgens">Vanessa Hudgens</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Adrienne_Bailon" class="mw-redirect" title="Adrienne Bailon">Adrienne Bailon</a> have been victims of such abuses of sexting.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2008 survey by The National Campaign to Prevent Teen and Unplanned Pregnancy and CosmoGirl.com<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> suggested a trend of sexting and other seductive online content being readily shared between teens. One in five teen girls surveyed (22 per cent)—and 11 per cent of teen girls aged 13–16 years old—say they have electronically sent, or posted online, nude or semi-nude images of themselves. One-third (33 per cent) of teen boys and one-quarter (25 per cent) of teen girls say they were shown private nude or semi-nude images. According to the survey, sexually suggestive messages (text, e-mail, and instant messaging) were even more common than images, with 39 per cent of teens having sent or posted such messages, and half of the teens (50 per cent) having received them.
</p><p>A 2012 study that has received wide international media attention was conducted at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Utah" title="University of Utah">University of Utah</a> Department of Psychology by Donald S. Strassberg, Ryan Kelly McKinnon, Michael Sustaíta and Jordan Rullo. They surveyed 606 teenagers ages 14–18 and found that nearly 20 per cent of the students said they had sent a sexually explicit image of themselves via cell phone, and nearly twice as many said that they had received a sexually explicit picture. Of those receiving such a picture, over 25 per cent indicated that they had forwarded it to others. In addition, of those who had sent a sexually explicit picture, over a third had done so despite believing that there could be serious legal and other consequences if they got caught. Students who had sent a picture by cell phone were more likely than others to find the activity acceptable. The authors conclude: "These results argue for educational efforts such as cell phone safety assemblies, awareness days, integration into class curriculum and teacher training, designed to raise awareness about the potential consequences of sexting among young people."<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (December 2022)">excessive citations</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Sexting becomes a legal issue when teens (under 18) are involved, because any nude photos they may send of themselves would put the recipients in possession of child pornography.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_schools">In schools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: In schools"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Textinginclass.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Textinginclass.jpg/220px-Textinginclass.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Textinginclass.jpg/330px-Textinginclass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Textinginclass.jpg/440px-Textinginclass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="401" /></a><figcaption>Two girls text during class at school.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Text messaging has affected students academically by creating an easier way to cheat on exams. In December 2002, a dozen students were caught cheating on an accounting exam through the use of text messages on their mobile phones.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In December 2002, Hitotsubashi University in Japan failed 26 students for receiving emailed exam answers on their mobile phones.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The number of students caught using mobile phones to cheat on exams has increased significantly in recent years. According to Okada (2005), most Japanese mobile phones can send and receive long text messages of between 250 and 3000 characters with graphics, video, audio, and Web links.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In England, 287 school and college students were excluded from exams in 2004 for using mobile phones during exams.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Some teachers and professors claim that advanced texting features can lead to students cheating on exams.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Students in high school and college classrooms are using their mobile phones to send and receive texts during lectures at high rates. Further, published research has established that students who text during college lectures have impaired memories of the lecture material compared to students who do not.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> For example, in one study, the number of irrelevant text messages sent and received during a lecture covering the topic of developmental psychology was related to students' memory of the lecture.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bullying">Bullying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Bullying"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cyberbullying" title="Cyberbullying">Cyberbullying</a></div>
<p>Spreading rumors and gossip by text message, using text messages to <a href="/wiki/Bully" class="mw-redirect" title="Bully">bully</a> individuals, or forwarding texts that contain defamatory content is an issue of great concern for parents and schools. Text "bullying" of this sort can cause distress and damage reputations. In some cases, individuals who are bullied online have committed suicide. Harding and Rosenberg (2005) argue that the urge to forward text messages can be difficult to resist, describing text messages as "loaded weapons".<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The default messaging app for the <a href="/wiki/IPhone" title="IPhone">iPhone</a>, called <a href="/wiki/IMessage" title="IMessage">iMessage</a>, uses a data connection to send messages to other iMessage users, and leverages SMS as a fallback when no data connection is present, or when messaging non-iMessage users. The app changes the color of the message depending on which technology was used. This has led to instances of iMessage users bullying people without iPhones.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_perceptions_of_the_student">Influence on perceptions of the student</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Influence on perceptions of the student"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>When a student sends an email that contains phonetic abbreviations and acronyms that are common in text messaging (e.g., "gr8" instead of "great"), it can influence how that student is subsequently evaluated. In a study by Lewandowski and Harrington (2006), participants read a student's email sent to a professor that either contained text-messaging abbreviations (gr8, How R U?) or parallel text in standard English (great, How are you?), and then provided impressions of the sender.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Students who used abbreviations in their email were perceived as having a less favorable personality and as putting forth less effort on an essay they submitted along with the email. Specifically, abbreviation users were seen as less intelligent, responsible, motivated, studious, dependable, and hard-working. These findings suggest that the nature of a student's email communication can influence how others perceive the student and their work.
</p><p>However, students have become aware of the reality that using these <a href="/wiki/SMS_language" title="SMS language">textisms</a> and adaptations can negatively impact their professionalism. Drouin and Davis surveyed American undergraduates in 2009 and found that three quarters of participants believed the use of textisms were not appropriate in formal messaging and writing.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> A study performed by Grace et. al. (2013) asked 150 undergraduate students to rate the appropriateness of using textisms in a given scenario on a scale of one to five - five being entirely appropriate and one being not at all.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> All but eleven of the students rated the use of textisms in exams and typed assignments as “not at all appropriate,” showing that the students are aware of how they must adapt their written language and tone depending on the context.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Grace et. al. (2010) went further, observing hundreds of academic papers from previous undergraduate students' exams, only to find that out of 533,500 words, a mere 0.02% were textisms. They owe this to the fact that the more accumulated experience a student has, the more they are able to understand when the “appropriate” and “inappropriate” times to use such language is.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_crime">Law and crime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Law and crime"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Text messaging has been a subject of interest for police forces around the world. One of the issues of concern to <a href="/wiki/Law_enforcement" title="Law enforcement">law enforcement</a> agencies is the use of <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encrypted</a> text messages. In 2003, a British company developed a program called Fortress SMS which used 128 bit AES <a href="/wiki/Encryption" title="Encryption">encryption</a> to protect SMS messages.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Police have also retrieved deleted text messages to aid them in solving crimes. For example, Swedish police retrieved deleted texts from a cult member who claimed she committed a double murder based on forwarded texts she received.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> Police in <a href="/wiki/Tilburg" title="Tilburg">Tilburg</a>, Netherlands, started an SMS alert program, in which they would send a message to ask citizens to be vigilant when a burglar was on the loose or a child was missing in their neighbourhood. Several thieves have been caught and children have been found using the SMS Alerts. The service has been expanding to other cities.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> A Malaysian–Australian company has released a multi-layer SMS security program.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Boston police are now turning to text messaging to help stop crime. The Boston Police Department asks citizens to send texts to make anonymous crime tips.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under some interpretations of <a href="/wiki/Sharia_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharia law">sharia law</a>, husbands can divorce their wives by the pronouncement of <a href="/wiki/Talaq" class="mw-redirect" title="Talaq">talaq</a>. In 2003, a court in Malaysia upheld such a divorce pronouncement which was transmitted via SMS.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Supreme_Judicial_Court" title="Massachusetts Supreme Judicial Court">Massachusetts Supreme Judicial Court</a> ruled in 2017 that under the state constitution, police require a <a href="/wiki/Search_warrant" title="Search warrant">warrant</a> before obtaining access to text messages without consent.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_unrest">Social unrest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Social unrest"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Texting has been used on a number of occasions with the result of the gathering of large aggressive crowds. SMS messaging drew a crowd to <a href="/wiki/Cronulla_Beach" title="Cronulla Beach">Cronulla Beach</a> in Sydney resulting in the <a href="/wiki/2005_Cronulla_riots" title="2005 Cronulla riots">2005 Cronulla riots</a>. Not only were text messages circulating in the Sydney area but in other states as well (<i>Daily Telegraph</i>). The volume of such text messages and e-mails also increased in the wake of the riot.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The crowd of 5000 at stages became violent, attacking certain ethnic groups. Sutherland Shire Mayor directly blamed heavily circulated SMS messages for the unrest.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> NSW police considered whether people could be charged over the texting.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Retaliatory attacks also used SMS.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Narre Warren Incident, when a group of 500 party goers attended a party at Narre Warren in Melbourne, Australia, and rioted in January 2008, also was a response of communication being spread by SMS and Myspace.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Following the incident, the Police Commissioner wrote an open letter asking young people to be aware of the power of SMS and the Internet.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In Hong Kong, government officials find that text messaging helps socially because they can send multiple texts to the community. Officials say it is an easy way of contacting the community or individuals for meetings or events.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Texting was used to coordinate gatherings during the <a href="/wiki/2009_Iranian_election_protests" class="mw-redirect" title="2009 Iranian election protests">2009 Iranian election protests</a>.
</p><p>Between 2009 and 2012 the U.S. secretly created and funded a <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a>-like service for Cubans called <a href="/wiki/ZunZuneo" title="ZunZuneo">ZunZuneo</a>, initially based on mobile phone text message service and later with an internet interface. The service was funded by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Agency_for_International_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Agency for International Development">U.S. Agency for International Development</a> through its <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Transition_Initiatives" title="Office of Transition Initiatives">Office of Transition Initiatives</a>, who utilized contractors and front companies in the Cayman Islands, Spain and Ireland. A longer-term objective was to organize "smart mobs" that might "renegotiate the balance of power between the state and society." A database about the subscribers was created, including gender, age, and "political tendencies". At its peak ZunZuneo had 40,000 Cuban users, but the service closed as financially unsustainable when U.S. funding was stopped.<sup id="cite_ref-wp-20140403_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wp-20140403-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-forbes-20140404_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes-20140404-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_politics">In politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: In politics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/220px-Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/330px-Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg/440px-Threatening_text_-_Flickr_-_Al_Jazeera_English.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="533" /></a><figcaption>A text message that (he says) promises 500 <a href="/wiki/Libyan_dinar" title="Libyan dinar">Libyan dinars</a> ($400) to anyone who "makes noise" in support of <a href="/wiki/Gaddafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Gaddafi">Gaddafi</a> in the coming days</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Sms.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sms.jpg/220px-Sms.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="115" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Sms.jpg/330px-Sms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/Sms.jpg 2x" data-file-width="390" data-file-height="204" /></a><figcaption>A recruitment ban in French <a href="/wiki/SMS_language" title="SMS language">SMS language</a>: «Slt koi29 on é jamé 2tro @ s batre pour la P.&#160;;-)» = «<i>Salut! Quoi de neuf? On n'est jamais de trop à se battre pour la Paix!»</i></figcaption></figure>
<p>Text messaging has affected the political world. American campaigns find that text messaging is a much easier, cheaper way of getting to the voters than the door-to-door approach.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In 2006 Mexico's then president-elect <a href="/wiki/Felipe_Calder%C3%B3n" title="Felipe Calderón">Felipe Calderón</a> launched millions of text messages in the days immediately preceding his narrow win over <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9s_Manuel_L%C3%B3pez_Obrador" title="Andrés Manuel López Obrador">Andrés Manuel López Obrador</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In January 2001, Joseph Estrada was forced to resign from the post of president of the Philippines. The popular campaign against him was widely reported to have been coordinated with SMS chain letters.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> A massive texting campaign was credited with boosting youth turnout in Spain's 2004 parliamentary elections.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_156-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, Detroit Mayor Kwame Kilpatrick and his Chief of Staff at the time became entangled in a sex scandal stemming from the exchange of over 14,000 text messages that eventually led to his forced resignation, the conviction of perjury, and other charges.<sup id="cite_ref-huliq1_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huliq1-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
Text messaging has been used to turn down other political leaders. During the 2004 U.S. Democratic and Republican National Conventions, protesters used an SMS-based organizing tool called TXTmob to get to opponents.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In the last day before the 2004 presidential elections in Romania, a message against <a href="/wiki/Adrian_N%C4%83stase" title="Adrian Năstase">Adrian Năstase</a> was largely circulated, thus breaking the laws that prohibited campaigning that day. Text messaging has helped politics by promoting campaigns.
</p><p>On 20 January 2001, President <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Estrada" title="Joseph Estrada">Joseph Estrada</a> of the Philippines became the first head of state in history to lose power to a <a href="/wiki/Smart_mob" title="Smart mob">smart mob</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rheingold,_Howard_2002_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rheingold,_Howard_2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> More than one million Manila residents assembled at the site of the 1986 <a href="/wiki/People_Power_Revolution" title="People Power Revolution">People Power</a> peaceful demonstrations that have toppled the Marcos regime. These people have organized themselves and coordinated their actions through text messaging. They were able to bring down a government without having to use any weapons or violence. Through text messaging, their plans and ideas were communicated to others and successfully implemented. Also, this move encouraged the military to withdraw their support from the regime, and as a result, the Estrada government fell.<sup id="cite_ref-Rheingold,_Howard_2002_67-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rheingold,_Howard_2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> People were able to converge and unite with the use of their cell phones. "The rapid assembly of the anti-Estrada crowd was a hallmark of early <a href="/wiki/Smart_mob" title="Smart mob">smart mob</a> technology, and the millions of text messages exchanged by the demonstrators in 2001 was, by all accounts, a key to the crowds <a href="/wiki/Esprit_de_corps" class="mw-redirect" title="Esprit de corps">esprit de corps</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Rheingold,_Howard_2002_67-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rheingold,_Howard_2002-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_in_healthcare">Use in healthcare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Use in healthcare"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png/220px-Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png/330px-Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png/440px-Text_messaging_used_to_provide_encouragement_to_quit_smoking.png 2x" data-file-width="1794" data-file-height="1189" /></a><figcaption>Some health organizations manage text messaging services to help people avoid smoking.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Text messaging is a rapidly growing trend in Healthcare.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2014)">when?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A randomized controlled trial of text messaging intervention for diabetes in Bangladesh was one of the first robust trials to report improvement in diabetes management in a low-and-middle income country.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> A recent systematic review and individual participants data meta analysis from 3779 participants reported that mobile phone text messaging could improve blood pressure and body mass index.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Another study in people with type 2 diabetes showed that participants were willing to pay a modest amount to receive a diabetes text messaging program in addition to standard care.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> "One survey found that 73% of physicians text other physicians about work- similar to the overall percentage of the population that texts."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> A 2006 study of reminder messages sent to children and adolescents with <a href="/wiki/Type_1_diabetes_mellitus" class="mw-redirect" title="Type 1 diabetes mellitus">type 1 diabetes mellitus</a> showed favorable changes in adherence to treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> A risk is that these physicians could be violating the <a href="/wiki/Health_Insurance_Portability_and_Accountability_Act" title="Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act">Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act</a>. Where messages could be saved to a phone indefinitely, patient information could be subject to theft or loss, and could be seen by other unauthorized persons. The HIPAA privacy rule requires that any text message involving a medical decision must be available for the patient to access, meaning that any texts that are not documented in an EMR system could be a HIPAA violation.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_concerns">Medical concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Medical concerns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/BlackBerry_thumb" class="mw-redirect" title="BlackBerry thumb">BlackBerry thumb</a></div>
<p>The excessive use of the thumb for pressing keys on mobile devices has led to a high rate of a form of <a href="/wiki/Repetitive_strain_injury" title="Repetitive strain injury">repetitive strain injury</a> termed "BlackBerry thumb" (although this refers to strain developed on older Blackberry devices, which had a scroll wheel on the side of the phone). An inflammation of the tendons in the thumb caused by constant text-messaging is also called text-messager's thumb, or texting <a href="/wiki/Tenosynovitis" title="Tenosynovitis">tenosynovitis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-test_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-test-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Texting has also been linked as a secondary source in numerous traffic collisions, in which police investigations of mobile phone records have found that many drivers have lost control of their cars while attempting to send or retrieve a text message. Increasing cases of Internet <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_addiction" title="Behavioral addiction">addiction</a> are now also being linked to text messaging, as mobile phones are now more likely to have e-mail and Web capabilities to complement the ability to text.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Etiquette">Etiquette</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Etiquette"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Texting etiquette refers to what is considered appropriate texting behaviour. These expectations may concern different areas, such as the context in which a text was sent and received/read, who each participant was with when the participant sent or received/read a text message or what constitutes impolite text messages.<sup id="cite_ref-Shuter_2010_123_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuter_2010_123-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> At the website of <a href="/wiki/The_Emily_Post_Institute" title="The Emily Post Institute">The Emily Post Institute</a>, the topic of <i>texting</i> has spurred several articles with the "do's and dont's" regarding the new form of communication. One example from the site is: "Keep your message brief. No one wants to have an entire conversation with you by texting when you could just call him or her instead."<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Another example is: "Don't use all Caps. Typing a text message in all capital letters will appear as though you are shouting at the recipient, and should be avoided."
</p><p>Expectations for etiquette may differ depending on various factors. For example, expectations for appropriate behaviour have been found to differ markedly between the U.S. and India.<sup id="cite_ref-Shuter_2010_123_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shuter_2010_123-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Another example is generational differences. In <i>The M-Factor: How the Millennial Generation Is Rocking the Workplace</i>, Lynne Lancaster and David Stillman note that younger Americans often do not consider it rude to answer their cell or begin texting in the middle of a face-to-face conversation with someone else, while older people, less used to the behavior and the accompanying lack of eye contact or attention, find this to be disruptive and ill-mannered.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> With regard to texting in the workplace, <a href="/wiki/Plantronics" title="Plantronics">Plantronics</a> studied how we communicate at work] and found that 58% of US <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_workers" class="mw-redirect" title="Knowledge workers">knowledge workers</a> have increased the use of text messaging for work in the past five years.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The same study found that 33% of knowledge workers felt text messaging was critical or very important to success and productivity at work.<sup id="cite_ref-alison_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alison-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Typing_awareness_indicators">Typing awareness indicators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Typing awareness indicators"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Left_on_read.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Left_on_read.jpg/220px-Left_on_read.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="119" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Left_on_read.jpg/330px-Left_on_read.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Left_on_read.jpg/440px-Left_on_read.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2022" data-file-height="1094" /></a><figcaption>Mural of a text message reading "I love you" and an ellipsis as a typing awareness indicator on the left (France, 2021)</figcaption></figure>
<p>In some text messaging software products, an <a href="/wiki/Ellipsis" title="Ellipsis">ellipsis</a> is displayed while the interlocutor is typing characters. The feature has been referred to as a "typing awareness indicator",<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> for which patents have been filed since the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Challenges">Challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Challenges"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spam">Spam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Spam"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_spam" title="Mobile phone spam">Mobile phone spam</a></div>
<p>In 2002, an increasing trend towards <a href="/wiki/Mobile_phone_spam" title="Mobile phone spam">spamming</a> mobile phone users through SMS prompted cellular-service carriers to take steps against the practice, before it became a widespread problem. No major spamming incidents involving SMS had been reported as of March 2007<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, but the existence of mobile phone spam<sup id="cite_ref-3750_FREEMSG_Scam_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3750_FREEMSG_Scam-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> has been noted by industry watchdogs including <i><a href="/wiki/Consumer_Reports" title="Consumer Reports">Consumer Reports</a></i> magazine and the Utility Consumers' Action Network (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.ucan.org/">UCAN</a>). In 2005, UCAN brought a case against Sprint for spamming its customers and charging $0.10 per text message.<sup id="cite_ref-Sprint_spam_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sprint_spam-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> The case was settled in 2006 with Sprint agreeing not to send customers Sprint advertisements via SMS.<sup id="cite_ref-Sprint_spam_result_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sprint_spam_result-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> SMS expert Acision (formerly LogicaCMG Telecoms) reported a new type of SMS malice at the end of 2006, noting the first instances of SMiShing (a cousin to e-mail <a href="/wiki/Phishing" title="Phishing">phishing</a> scams). In SMiShing, users receive SMS messages posing to be from a company, enticing users to phone premium-rate numbers or reply with personal information. Similar concerns were reported by PhonepayPlus, a <a href="/wiki/Consumer_protection_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Consumer protection in the United Kingdom">consumer watchdog</a> in the United Kingdom, in 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pricing_concerns">Pricing concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Pricing concerns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Concerns have been voiced<sup id="cite_ref-crunchgear.com_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crunchgear.com-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> over the excessive cost of off-plan text messaging in the United States. <a href="/wiki/AT%26T_Mobility" title="AT&amp;T Mobility">AT&amp;T Mobility</a>, along with most other service providers, charges texters 20 cents per message if they do not have a messaging plan or if they have exceeded their allotted number of texts. Given that an SMS message is at most 160 <a href="/wiki/Bytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Bytes">bytes</a> in size, this cost scales to a cost of $1,310<sup id="cite_ref-crunchgear.com_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crunchgear.com-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> per megabyte sent via text message. This is in sharp contrast with the price of unlimited data plans offered by the same carriers, which allow the transmission of hundreds of megabytes of data for monthly prices of about $15 to $45 in addition to a voice plan. As a comparison, a one-minute phone call uses up the same amount of network capacity as 600 text messages,<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> meaning that if the same cost-per-traffic formula were applied to phone calls, cell phone calls would cost $120 per minute. With service providers gaining more customers and expanding their capacity, their overhead costs should be decreasing, not increasing. In 2005, text messaging generated nearly 70 billion dollars in revenue, as reported by Gartner, industry analysts, three times as much as Hollywood box office sales in 2005. World figures showed that over a trillion text messages were sent in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although major cellphone providers deny any collusion, fees for out-of-package text messages have increased, doubling from 10 to 20 cents in the United States between 2007 and 2008 alone.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> On 16 July 2009, Senate hearings were held to look into any breach of the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Antitrust_Act" title="Sherman Antitrust Act">Sherman Antitrust Act</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> The same trend is visible in other countries, though increasingly widespread flat-rate plans, for example in Germany, do make text messaging easier, text messages sent abroad still result in higher costs.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Increasing_competition">Increasing competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Increasing competition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While text messaging is still a growing market, traditional SMS is becoming increasingly challenged by alternative messaging services which are available on smartphones with data connections. These services are much cheaper and offer more functionality like exchanging multimedia content (e.g. photos, videos or audio notes) and group messaging. Especially in western countries some of these services attract more and more users.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> Prominent examples of these include <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>'s <a href="/wiki/IMessage" title="IMessage">iMessage</a> (exclusive to the Apple ecosystem) and <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rich_Communication_Services" title="Rich Communication Services">RCS</a>.
</p><p>In 2021, 8.4 trillion SMS messages were sent globally, compared to 18.25 trillion for <a href="/wiki/WhatsApp" title="WhatsApp">WhatsApp</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_concerns">Security concerns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Security concerns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Experts have advised business users not to use consumer SMS for confidential communication. The contents of common SMS messages are known to the network operator's systems and personnel. Therefore, consumer SMS is not an appropriate technology for secure communications.<sup id="cite_ref-Gartner_Research_Analytical_Source_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gartner_Research_Analytical_Source-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> To address this issue, many companies use an <a href="/wiki/SMS_gateway" title="SMS gateway">SMS gateway</a> provider based on <a href="/wiki/Signaling_System_7" class="mw-redirect" title="Signaling System 7">SS7</a> connectivity to route the messages. The advantage of this international termination model is the ability to route data directly through <a href="/wiki/Signaling_System_7" class="mw-redirect" title="Signaling System 7">SS7</a>, which gives the provider visibility of the complete path of the SMS. This means SMS messages can be sent directly to and from recipients without having to go through the <a href="/wiki/Short_Message_Service_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Short Message Service Centre">SMS-C</a> of other mobile operators. This approach reduces the number of mobile operators that handle the message; however, experts have advised not to consider it as an end-to-end secure communication, as the content of the message is exposed to the <a href="/wiki/SMS_gateway" title="SMS gateway">SMS gateway</a> provider.
</p><p>An alternative approach is to use end-to-end security software that runs on both the sending and receiving device, where the original text message is transmitted in encrypted form as a consumer SMS. By using key rotation, the encrypted text messages stored under <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications_data_retention" class="mw-redirect" title="Telecommunications data retention">data retention</a> laws at the network operator cannot be decrypted even if one of the devices is compromised. A problem with this approach is that communicating devices needs to run compatible software. Failure rates without backward notification can be high between carriers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> International texting can be unreliable depending on the country of origin, destination and respective operators. Differences in the <a href="/wiki/Character_set" class="mw-redirect" title="Character set">character sets</a> used for coding can cause a text message sent from one country to another to become unreadable.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Records_and_competition">Records and competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Records and competition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_World_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinness Book of World Records">Guinness Book of World Records</a></i> has a world record for text messaging, currently held by Sonja Kristiansen of Norway. Kristiansen keyed in the official text message, as established by Guinness, in 37.28 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-tv2nyhetene.no_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tv2nyhetene.no-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The message is, "The razor-toothed piranhas of the genera Serrasalmus and Pygocentrus are the most ferocious freshwater fish in the world. In reality, they seldom attack a human."<sup id="cite_ref-tv2nyhetene.no_183-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tv2nyhetene.no-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, the record was held by a 24-year-old Scottish man, Craig Crosbie, who completed the same message in 48 seconds, beating the previous time by 19 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> <i>The Book of Alternative Records</i> lists Chris Young of <a href="/wiki/Salem,_Oregon" title="Salem, Oregon">Salem, Oregon</a>, as the world-record holder for the fastest 160-character text message where the contents of the message are not provided ahead of time. His record of 62.3 seconds was set on 23 May 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Elliot Nicholls of Dunedin, New Zealand, currently holds the world record for the fastest blindfolded text messaging. A record of a 160-letter text in 45 seconds while blindfolded was set on 17 November 2007, beating the old record of 1-minute 26 seconds set by an Italian in September 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Ohio native Andrew Acklin is credited with the world record for most text messages sent or received in a single month, with 200,052. His accomplishments were first in the World Records Academy and later followed up by <i>Ripley's Believe It Or Not 2010: Seeing Is Believing</i>. He has been acknowledged by The Universal Records Database for the most text messages in a single month; however, this has since been broken twice and as of 2010 was listed as 566607 messages by Fred Lindgren.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In January 2010, <a href="/wiki/LG_Electronics" title="LG Electronics">LG Electronics</a> sponsored an international competition, the <a href="/wiki/LG_Mobile_World_Cup" title="LG Mobile World Cup">LG Mobile World Cup</a>, to determine the fastest pair of texters. The winners were a team from South Korea, Ha Mok-min and Bae Yeong-ho.<sup id="cite_ref-lgmobileworldcup_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lgmobileworldcup-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> On 6 April 2011, SKH Apps released an iPhone app, iTextFast, to allow consumers to test their texting speed and practice the paragraph used by <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_World_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinness Book of World Records">Guinness Book of World Records</a></i>. As of 2011, best time listed on Game Center for that paragraph is 34.65 seconds.<sup id="cite_ref-iTextFast_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iTextFast-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Morse_code">Morse code</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Morse code"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A few competitions have been held between expert <a href="/wiki/Morse_code" title="Morse code">Morse code</a> operators and expert SMS users.<sup id="cite_ref-Morse_race_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morse_race-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> Several mobile phones have Morse code ring tones and alert messages. For example, many <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a> mobile phones have an option to beep "S M S" in Morse code when it receives a short message. Some of these phones could also play the Nokia slogan "Connecting people" in Morse code as a message tone.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> There are third-party applications available for some mobile phones that allow Morse input for short messages.<sup id="cite_ref-Nokia_Morse_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nokia_Morse-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-O&#39;Reilly_Morse_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-O&#39;Reilly_Morse-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-engadget_Morse_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engadget_Morse-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Tattle_texting">Tattle texting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Tattle texting"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>"Tattle texting" can mean either of two different texting trends:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arena_security">Arena security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Arena security"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many sports arenas now offer a number where patrons can text report security concerns, like drunk or unruly fans, or safety issues like spills.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> These programs have been praised by patrons and security personnel as more effective than traditional methods. For instance, the patron doesn't need to leave his seat and miss the event in order to report something important. Also, disruptive fans can be reported with relative anonymity. "Text tattling" also gives security personnel a useful tool to prioritize messages. For instance, a single complaint in one section about an unruly fan can be addressed when convenient, while multiple complaints by several different patrons can be acted upon immediately.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Smart_cars">Smart cars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Text_messaging&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Smart cars"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In this context, "tattle texting" refers to an automatic text sent by the computer in an automobile, because a preset condition was met.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The most common use for this is for parents to receive texts from the car their child is driving, alerting them to speeding or other issues. Employers can also use the service to monitor their corporate vehicles. The technology is still new and (currently) only available on a few car models.
</p><p>Common conditions that can be chosen to send a text are:
</p>
<ul><li><b>Speeding.</b> With the use of <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a>, stored maps, and <a href="/wiki/Speed_limit" title="Speed limit">speed limit</a> information, the onboard computer can determine if the driver is exceeding the current speed limit. The device can store this information and/or send it to another recipient.</li>
<li><b>Range.</b> Parents/employers can set a maximum range from a fixed location after which a "tattle text" is sent. Not only can this keep children close to home and keep employees from using corporate vehicles inappropriately, but it can also be a crucial tool for quickly identifying stolen vehicles, car jackings, and kidnappings.</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1133582631"><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://theweek.com/article/index/237240/the-text-message-turns-20-a-brief-history-of-sms">"The Text Message Turns 20: A Brief History of SMS"</a>. <i>theweek.com</i>. 3 December 2012<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2 February</span> 2016</span>. <q>1984 [...] Sitting at a typewriter at his home in Bonn, Germany, Friedhelm Hillebrand types random sentences and questions, counting every letter, number, and space. Almost every time, the messages amount to fewer than 160 characters — what would become the limit of early text messages — and thus the concept for the perfect-length, rapid-fire 'short message' was born.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=theweek.com&amp;rft.atitle=The+Text+Message+Turns+20%3A+A+Brief+History+of+SMS&amp;rft.date=2012-12-03&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Ftheweek.com%2Farticle%2Findex%2F237240%2Fthe-text-message-turns-20-a-brief-history-of-sms&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AText+messaging" class="Z3988"></span></span>
